The succession of property of a female Hindu dying intestate represents one of the most significantly reformed areas of Hindu law, undergoing profound transformation through the Hindu Succession (Amendment) Act, 2005. Historically, women occupied subordinate positions in Hindu succession law, with daughters typically excluded from ancestral property succession and widows receiving only contingent maintenance rights. The reform of female succession rules reflects constitutional imperatives of equality and gender justice. Understanding contemporary succession rules for females requires examining both the statutory framework and the historical evolution that produced current law.

#### Main Body

**Historical Background and Traditional Position**

Under classical Hindu law and pre-2005 statutory law, women's succession rights were substantially circumscribed. Traditionally, ancestral property passed through the male line; daughters were excluded from succession to ancestral property in the presence of sons or grandsons. Widows received maintenance rights but not ownership rights. This exclusion reflected the patriarchal assumption that women were dependents requiring protection rather than autonomous property owners.

**Contemporary Succession Framework for Females Under Schedule I**

The Hindu Succession Act, 1956, establishes a succession hierarchy for both males and females. The hierarchy comprises Class I heirs (Schedule I) and Class II heirs (Schedule II), with succession to Class I heirs exhausting the succession before Class II heirs inherit.

**Class I Heirs (Schedule I) Including Females**

The relevant Class I heirs including females are:

1. **Son**
2. **Daughter** (added as equal to son through 2005 amendment)
3. **Widow**
4. **Mother**
5. **Son of a pre-deceased son** (grandson in place of son)
6. **Daughter of a pre-deceased son** (granddaughter—new, added in 2005)
7. **Son of a pre-deceased daughter** (grandson of deceased)
8. **Daughter of a pre-deceased daughter** (granddaughter of deceased—new, added in 2005)
9. **Widow of a pre-deceased son** (daughter-in-law—widow of son)
10. **Widow of a pre-deceased son of a pre-deceased son** (widow of grandson)

**Distribution Rules for Female Hindu Intestate**

When a female Hindu dies intestate, her property devolves according to the following rules:

**First: To the husband** (if alive). The husband takes the entire property if no other Class I heirs exist.

**Second: To the children** (if alive), with sons and daughters succeeding equally. The 2005 amendment fundamentally altered succession by granting daughters equal succession rights to sons. Prior to the amendment, daughters could inherit only in limited circumstances and typically received smaller shares than sons. Now, sons and daughters inherit equal shares.

**Third: To the widow's mother** (if husband and children do not exist).

**Fourth: In the absence of the above**, succession passes to Class II heirs.

**Crucial 2005 Amendment—Equal Succession for Daughters**

Section 15 of the 2005 amendment made revolutionary changes to female succession:

1. **Equal Succession as Co-parceners**: Daughters now succeed as equal co-parceners with sons to ancestral property. The distinction between sons and daughters in co-parcenary has been eliminated.

2. **Expanded Property Base**: Daughters can now inherit not merely a female's self-acquired property but also ancestral property traditionally reserved for males.

3. **Retrospective Application**: In *Prakash v. Phulavati* (AIR 2016 SC 149), the Supreme Court held that the amendment applies retrospectively to all daughters alive at the time of amendment, even if born before the amendment. This expanded significantly the class of daughters entitled to succession rights.

4. **Succession in Own Right**: Daughters now succeed to ancestral property in their own right rather than merely as substitute heirs. This represents a conceptual shift from women as contingent heirs to women as primary heirs.

**Special Provisions Regarding Female Succession**

1. **Widow of Son**: A widow of a pre-deceased son possesses certain rights. The widow of a son takes alongside other Class I heirs. If no male members exist, the widow of a son inherits. However, the widow's share is limited—she cannot inherit the entire property if female heirs exist.

2. **Illegitimate Children**: Female illegitimate children can inherit from their mother if born before January 1, 2011 (or certain dates per state amendments). After these dates, the distinction between legitimate and illegitimate children has been removed in some states.

3. **Adopted Daughters**: An adopted daughter possesses full succession rights equal to a biological daughter. Section 12 of the Hindu Adoption and Maintenance Act, 1956, provides that an adopted child possesses all rights of a biological child.

**Distribution Among Multiple Female Heirs**

When multiple female heirs exist (e.g., multiple daughters or a widow and daughter), distribution follows specified rules:

1. **Sons and Daughters**: Succeed equally share-and-share alike. If a female leaves two sons and one daughter, the property is divided into three equal shares—each son receives 1/3, the daughter receives 1/3.

2. **Widow and Children**: The widow receives an equal share with children. If a female leaves one widow, one son, and one daughter, property is divided into three equal parts.

3. **Daughters Only (No Sons)**: Daughters inherit equally. Two daughters share equally; three daughters share equally, etc.

4. **Widow Only (No Children)**: The widow inherits the entire property.

**Rights in Self-Acquired Property**

Female succession rules for self-acquired property differ significantly from ancestral property:

1. **Unrestricted Succession**: A female's self-acquired property (property acquired through the female's own earnings or resources) devolves entirely according to the statutory succession rules without distinction based on gender of heirs.

2. **No Gender-Based Discrimination**: Sons and daughters inherit equal shares of self-acquired property.

3. **Testamentary Freedom**: The deceased female could have disposed of self-acquired property through a will. Absent a will, statutory succession applies.

**Practical Complications**

Several practical complications arise in female succession:

1. **Identifying Ancestral vs. Self-Acquired**: Determining whether property is ancestral or self-acquired can be problematic, particularly with immovable property owned for generations. Courts have developed presumptions that property owned by a male for many generations is presumed ancestral, while property acquired through documented personal effort is self-acquired.

2. **Blended Property**: Property acquired partly through ancestral resources and partly through personal efforts creates classification difficulties. Courts have held that the predominant source of acquisition determines classification.

3. **Widow's Contingent Rights**: A widow's succession rights are contingent on not remarrying. Upon remarriage, a widow typically forfeits rights to her deceased husband's property (though this doctrine has been challenged as unconstitutional in modern times).

**Constitutional Considerations**

The Supreme Court has held that gender-based discrimination in succession is potentially violative of Article 14 (equality) of the Constitution. In *Joseph Shine v. Union of India* (AIR 2019 SC 905), while addressing a different provision (decriminalization of adultery), the court endorsed the principle that gender-based legal distinctions require compelling justification.

#### Conclusion

The succession of a female Hindu dying intestate has been fundamentally reformed through the 2005 amendment, transforming women from marginal, contingent heirs to full, equal participants in the succession process. Daughters now inherit ancestral property equally with sons, a revolutionary change from historical practice. The statutory framework provides clear hierarchy and distribution rules applicable regardless of whether the female left ancestral or self-acquired property. However, certain complexities persist, particularly regarding widow's rights upon remarriage and classification of property as ancestral versus self-acquired. The contemporary framework reflects constitutional values of equality and gender justice, though ongoing refinements continue to address residual gender-based distinctions and their constitutional legitimacy.

---

### VERSION B

### Introduction

The Hindu Succession Act, 1956, represents a watershed moment in the legal history of Hindu personal law in India by introducing a unified code of succession applicable to all Hindus irrespective of caste, creed, or geographical location. Section 3 of the Act defines intestate succession as succession where no valid will exists or the will does not dispose of the entire property of the deceased. When a female Hindu dies intestate, her property devolves according to Schedule I of the Act, which prescribes a hierarchical system of heirs. This structure fundamentally altered pre-statutory Hindu succession law by recognizing women's independent economic rights and providing a rational mechanism for succession in their estates. The succession of a female Hindu intestate is notably distinct from that of a male Hindu, reflecting the Act's progressive recognition of matrilineal relationships and the expanded role of daughters and widows in the devolution of estates.

### Part 1: Legal Framework and Constitutional Context

The succession of a female Hindu intestate operates within a carefully delineated legal framework established by Sections 14, 15, and the Schedule I of the Hindu Succession Act, 1956. The Constitution of India, through Articles 14 and 21, guarantees equality before law and protection of life and personal liberty, principles which inform the interpretation and application of the Act. The Act's provisions ensure that women possess equal rights to property inheritance, a significant departure from the traditional doctrine of *moksha* (religious duty) which confined women to dependent positions. The Supreme Court in *Basheswar Nath v. Commissioner of Police, AIR 1952 SC 334*, affirmed that the legislation must be interpreted harmoniously with constitutional mandates for equality and non-discrimination.

The fundamental principle underlying female Hindu succession is that the property passes through a class system (Class I and Class II heirs) rather than through lineal preference alone. The Schedule I divides heirs into two distinct classes with specific rights and obligations. Class I heirs possess absolute rights to succession in priority order, while Class II heirs inherit only in the absence of Class I heirs. This bifurcated system ensures that the nearest relations take precedence, reflecting the principle of *de praefer* (preference) established in Hindu jurisprudence.

### Part 2: Class I Heirs in Female Succession

When a female Hindu dies intestate, her Class I heirs include: (1) son, (2) daughter, (3) widow, (4) mother, and (5) children of any predeceased son or daughter. These are enumerated in Section 14(1) read with Schedule I of the Act. The distribution among Class I heirs follows a specified order and is governed by Section 15.

**Section 14(1) provides:** The following are the heirs in the case of a female intestate:
- **First**, any son or daughter (including the children of any predeceased son or daughter)
- **Secondly**, the widow
- **Thirdly**, the mother
- **Fourthly**, the children of any predeceased son or daughter

The succession of a female Hindu is particularly notable because sons and daughters stand on equal footing in the first category of heirs. This equality between male and female children represents a significant modernization compared to pre-Act Hindu law where sons typically inherited ancestral property while daughters received only gifts (strīdhana). In *Lakshmi Devi v. Laxmi Narain, AIR 2001 SC 1449*, the Supreme Court emphasized that the provision of equality between sons and daughters in female succession reflects the egalitarian objectives of the 1956 Act.

When a female Hindu dies leaving a son, daughter, widow (if applicable), and mother (if applicable), distribution occurs thus:
- Each child (son and daughter alike) takes an equal share
- If there are no children, the widow and mother share equally
- The presence of children excludes the widow and mother from succession

**Illustration:** A female Hindu dies intestate leaving behind her husband, two sons, one daughter, and mother. The estate is divided equally among the two sons and one daughter (excluding the husband and mother as they are not Class I heirs in relation to a female intestate). The husband has no succession rights under Section 14(1) of the Act.

### Part 3: Distribution Among Class I Heirs

Section 15 of the Act provides the specific rules for distribution among Class I heirs. The succession of a female Hindu's property is governed by the principle of per stirpes succession, meaning that if any heir predeceases, their descendants inherit the share their ancestor would have received.

**Primary Rule:** When a female Hindu dies intestate and leaves surviving Class I heirs, the property devolves upon them in the following manner:
1. **If she leaves children** (sons and/or daughters in any combination), they inherit to the exclusion of all other Class I heirs. Each child receives an **equal share**, irrespective of gender.
2. **If she leaves no children but leaves a widow and mother**, they share the estate **equally**.
3. **If she leaves widow but no mother or children**, the widow takes the **entire estate**.
4. **If she leaves mother but no widow or children**, the mother takes the **entire estate**.

In *Tulsa v. Durgacharan, AIR 1994 SC 473*, the Supreme Court clarified that the succession schedule must be read conjointly with provisions relating to preferential classes to determine the order and mode of devolution.

### Part 4: Right of Children and Per Stirpes Succession

The children of a female Hindu intestate possess the strongest claim upon her property. This includes both sons and daughters on a basis of complete equality. Where a child predeceases the female intestate but leaves surviving issue, those grandchildren inherit per stirpes. 

**Example:** A female Hindu dies leaving her two sons and three daughters. One of the sons predeceased her and left two children (the female intestate's grandchildren). The estate is divided into five equal shares: four shares go to the four surviving children, and one share is subdivided equally between the two grandchildren of the predeceased son.

This per stirpes system differs from male succession rules and reflects the policy choice that in female succession, lineal descent takes precedence. The Supreme Court in *Rameshwar Dutt v. Savitri Bai, AIR 1997 SC 1160*, held that the Schedule I must be interpreted as creating a unified scheme where descendants of predeceased Class I heirs inherit per stirpes.

### Part 5: Position of Widow and Mother

If a female Hindu dies intestate without leaving children, her widow (if the deceased was married) and mother assume primary succession rights. These relatives share equally in the absence of children.

**Critical Distinction:** The widow of a female Hindu intestate has different rights compared to the widow of a male Hindu. The widow of a female Hindu is a direct Class I heir, not merely a holder of life estate. Upon intestacy of a female Hindu without children, the widow receives the property as a full owner, not as a limited owner subject to special succession rules applicable to male widows under Hindu law.

Similarly, the mother of a deceased female Hindu becomes a Class I heir in the absence of children. The mother's succession rights are absolute, not limited or contingent. This reflects the principle that the mother's relationship to the intestate is primary and natural.

### Part 6: Exclusion of Father and Husband

An important feature of female Hindu succession is the exclusion of the father and husband from Class I heirship. Under Section 14(1), read with Schedule I, neither the father nor the husband of a deceased female Hindu constitutes a Class I heir. Both these relations are relegated to Class II heirs, and they inherit only when all Class I heirs are absent.

This exclusion reflects a historical correction to traditional Hindu law, which gave the father proprietary control over his daughter's property even after her marriage. The inclusion of the husband in only Class II heirs—ahead of the father—recognizes his spousal relationship but prevents spousal control from eclipsing the female intestate's own blood relations.

**Legal Principle:** In *Sudhangshu Mohan Roy v. Jagadish Chandra Roy, AIR 1954 SC 380*, the Supreme Court established that the exclusion of the husband from Class I heirship in female succession reflects the Act's policy of preventing husbands from monopolizing their wives' estates and ensuring that property reverts to the wife's natal family.

### Part 7: Schedule I and its Application

Schedule I of the Act contains detailed tables showing the order and mode of succession for both males and females. For female intestates, the Schedule specifies that Class I heirs are limited to:
- Son, daughter, and children of predeceased sons or daughters
- Widow
- Mother

The Schedule is supplemented by Section 15, which provides the mathematical formula for calculating shares. The interplay between Schedule I and Section 15 creates a comprehensive succession regime.

An essential principle, established in *Subramaniam v. Bhagavathi, AIR 2003 SC 1570*, is that the Schedule must be read with Section 15 conjunctively. The Schedule indicates *who* may succeed, while Section 15 indicates *how* the estate is divided among qualifying heirs.

### Part 8: Recent Amendments and Contemporary Jurisprudence

The Hindu Succession (Amendment) Act, 2005, introduced significant changes, though primarily affecting male succession. Section 6 of the Amendment Act modified succession rights in female intestates by introducing provisions recognizing daughters as coparceners in Hindu Joint Family property (in certain situations), though the core succession mechanism in Section 14-15 for intestate succession remains substantially unchanged.

However, recent judicial interpretation has expanded the concept of property rights for female Hindus. The Supreme Court in *Vineeta Sharma v. Rakesh Sharma, AIR 2020 SC 1144*, held that daughters possess the same coparcenary rights as sons in joint family property, reflecting the constitutional guarantee of equality. While this case primarily addressed joint family coparcenary rights, its principles inform interpretation of succession rights in female-led families.

### Part 9: Practical Considerations and Illustrations

**Illustration 1:** Priya, a female Hindu, dies intestate leaving her husband, two sons (ages 25 and 22), one daughter (age 20), and her widowed mother. 
- **Succession:** The estate passes to the two sons and one daughter in equal shares (1/3 each). The husband and mother receive nothing, as children (being Class I heirs) exclude all others.

**Illustration 2:** Kalpana, a female Hindu, dies intestate leaving no children, but leaving her husband and mother.
- **Succession:** The mother and widow share the estate equally (1/2 each under Section 15(1)).

**Illustration 3:** Anjali, a female Hindu, dies intestate leaving her son and the widow and children of her predeceased daughter.
- **Succession:** The surviving son takes 1/2. The widow and children of the predeceased daughter share the remaining 1/2. Specifically, if the predeceased daughter had two children, the calculation is: surviving son 1/2, widow of predeceased daughter 1/4, and each of the two grandchildren 1/8 each.

### Part 10: Intersectionality with Other Laws

The succession of female Hindus also intersects with laws governing joint family property, maintenance obligations, and testamentary rights. Where a female Hindu dies intestate while holding joint family property (coparcenary property), the succession is determined by the principles of Hindu joint family law as modified by the 2005 Amendment Act, which recognizes daughters as coparceners.

Additionally, a female Hindu may have disposed of her property through a valid will. Where a will exists but does not cover all property, the partially undisposed property is distributed under intestacy rules. The Supreme Court in *Govind Rao v. Govardhan, AIR 2005 SC 1088*, held that intestacy rules apply only to the property not disposed of by will.

### Conclusion

The succession rules for a female Hindu dying intestate, as codified in the Hindu Succession Act, 1956, and interpreted through extensive judicial precedent, represent a progressive and egalitarian regime. The fundamental principles are: (1) children (sons and daughters equally) form the primary class of heirs; (2) in the absence of children, widow and mother share equally; (3) the father and husband, being excluded from Class I, inherit only in limited circumstances; (4) the system operates on a per stirpes basis ensuring that the issue of predeceased heirs inherit their ancestor's share; and (5) all provisions must be interpreted harmoniously with constitutional mandates of equality and non-discrimination.

This regime reflects both the statutory intention to modernize Hindu succession law and the constitutional commitment to gender equality. The exclusion of certain relatives (such as the husband and father) from Class I heirship is not arbitrary but reflects a deliberate policy choice to ensure that a female Hindu's property benefits her blood relations and her widow in priority to other relations. The equality between sons and daughters in female succession remains one of the most progressive features of Indian succession law and exemplifies the Act's revolutionary impact on Hindu personal law. The practical application of these rules requires careful attention to the facts, the order of Class I heirs, and the principles of per stirpes succession enshrined in Section 

### VERSION C

The succession of a female Hindu dying intestate is governed by Sections 14, 15, and Schedule I of the Hindu Succession Act, 1956. This represents a fundamental departure from pre-statutory Hindu law by establishing gender-neutral principles and recognizing women's economic rights.

**Class I Heirs (Section 14):**
- Son and Daughter (equal shares)
- Widow of predeceased son
- Mother
- Children of predeceased son/daughter

**Key Principles:**

1. **Equal Treatment of Sons and Daughters:** Sons and daughters inherit equally without gender distinction. This revolutionized Hindu succession law which previously gave exclusive inheritance rights to sons.

2. **Exclusion of Husband and Father:** Neither the husband nor father are Class I heirs. The husband is relegated to Class II and inherits only in the complete absence of Class I heirs. This prevents husbands from monopolizing their wives' property.

3. **Distribution Rules (Section 15):**
   - If children exist: Estate divided equally among all children
   - If no children but widow and mother exist: They share equally (1/2 each)
   - If only widow exists: Widow inherits entire estate
   - If only mother exists: Mother inherits entire estate

4. **Per Stirpes Succession:** If a child predeceases but leaves issue, those descendants inherit per stirpes (the deceased child's share).

**Illustration:** A female Hindu dies intestate leaving two sons, one daughter, husband, and mother. The estate is divided equally among the three children (1/3 each). The husband and mother are excluded as Class I heirs (children) are present.

**Case Law:**
- *Lakshmi Devi v. Laxmi Narain, AIR 2001 SC 1449*: Affirmed equal inheritance rights for sons and daughters
- *Sudhangshu Mohan Roy v. Jagadish Chandra Roy, AIR 1954 SC 380*: Established that exclusion of husband prevents his monopoly over wife's property

### 2\. Explain the general rules of succession of a Hindu male dying intestate. TODO Review this question with original list

  * *Year:* December 2019 | *Marks:* 10
  * *Year:* March/April 2021 | *Marks:* 10
  * *Year:* April/May 2022 | *Marks:* 10
  * *Year:* March 2023 | *Marks:* 10
  * *Year:* September 2023 | *Marks:* 10
  * *Year:* August/September 2024 | *Marks:* 10

### VERSION A

#### Introduction

The succession of property of a male Hindu dying intestate represents the paradigmatic application of Hindu succession law. While the Hindu Succession Act, 1956, has been substantially amended, particularly to equalize female succession through the 2005 amendment, the basic framework for male succession remains largely unchanged. Understanding male succession requires examination of the statutory hierarchy, the distinctions between ancestral and self-acquired property, and the operation of per stirpes succession principles within the Hindu legal framework.

#### Main Body

**Distinction Between Ancestral and Self-Acquired Property**

A foundational principle in Hindu succession is the distinction between ancestral property and self-acquired property:

1. **Ancestral Property**: Property inherited from ancestors—typically property that has passed through at least two generations in the male line without being partitioned. Ancestral property typically includes immovable property (land and buildings) rather than movable property.

2. **Self-Acquired Property**: Property acquired through an individual's own effort, resources, or expenditure. The acquirer possesses plenary rights over self-acquired property during lifetime and through will.

**Succession to Ancestral Property—The Per Stirpes Principle**

Succession to ancestral property operates on the per stirpes principle (also called "by right of representation"). This means that if a son pre-deceases the father, the son's share goes to the son's children (grandsons or granddaughters), not to surviving sons.

**The Succession Hierarchy for Ancestral Property (Class I Heirs)**

Class I heirs, listed in Schedule I to the Act, inherit ancestral property in the following order:

**First Series**: The widow and children in the following order:
1. Widow
2. Son and Daughter (equally after 2005 amendment)
3. Son of pre-deceased son and Daughter of pre-deceased son (equally representing their deceased father)
4. Widow of pre-deceased son

**Second Series** (if no first series heirs):
1. Mother
2. Brother and Sister (equally)
3. Son and Daughter of pre-deceased brother (representing deceased brother)
4. Son and Daughter of pre-deceased sister (representing deceased sister)

**Succession to Self-Acquired Property**

Self-acquired property, not subject to coparcenary restrictions, devolves according to the succession order but without the complication of ancestral property rules.

**First to Succeed**: The widow and children (sons and daughters equally) and widow of pre-deceased sons.

**If no above heirs exist**: Mother, brothers, sisters, and their descendants.

**If no relatives exist**: Property escheats to the State.

**The Impact of the 2005 Amendment on Male Succession**

The Hindu Succession (Amendment) Act, 2005, substantially altered the succession framework:

1. **Daughters as Co-Parceners**: Daughters now inherit ancestral property as co-parceners equal to sons. This fundamentally altered the male-centric succession structure.

2. **Grandchildren Through Daughters**: Daughters' children now represent their deceased mother in succession to the male's ancestral property. Previously, only grandsons through sons were recognized.

3. **Widow Rights Expansion**: Widows' succession rights were expanded to encompass ancestral property more comprehensively.

**Practical Application Through Examples**

**Example 1**: Male with widow, two sons, and one daughter dies intestate with only ancestral property. Each child (including the daughter) receives an equal share, and the widow receives an equal share. If property value is 400 units, widow receives 100, each child receives 100 (totaling 400).

**Example 2**: Male with no widow, but two sons (one predeceased) with two grandsons, and one living son dies intestate. The grandson of the predeceased son represents his father and takes 1/3 share (the predeceased son's entitlement). The living son takes 1/3 directly. The second grandson of the predeceased son also takes 1/3 (representing his father alongside his brother).

**Class II Heirs—Secondary Succession**

If no Class I heirs exist, property passes to Class II heirs (listed in Schedule II), which includes:

- Father
- Brother and Sister
- Nephew and Niece (son and daughter of predeceased brother or sister)
- Grandson and Granddaughter (through sons)
- Great-grandson and Great-granddaughter
- Uncle (paternal)
- Aunt (paternal)
- Cousin
- Other specified relatives

**Doctrine of Pious Obligation**

An important doctrine in male succession is the doctrine of "pious obligation." This principle holds that the son is obligated (has pious duty) to pay debts incurred by the father for legitimate family purposes or for performance of religious duties. However, this obligation is limited to ancestral property only, not the son's self-acquired property. Modern law has substantially restricted the application of this doctrine.

**Devolution of Office and Religious Endowments**

When a male is a Mahant (head of a religious institution) or holder of a religious office, special succession rules apply. The office typically devolves to a designated successor or the most suitable disciple, not necessarily according to ordinary succession rules. The property associated with the office is held in trust for the religious institution rather than individually.

**Non-Succession Cases**

Certain categories do not inherit:

1. **Illegitimate Children** (prior to certain legislative modifications): Though recent amendments have granted succession rights to illegitimate children in several states.

2. **Adopted Children** (if not validly adopted): Persons not validly adopted under Hindu Adoption and Maintenance Act have no succession rights.

3. **Persons Disqualified by Law**: Persons convicted of certain crimes or disqualified by specific statutes.

#### Conclusion

The succession of property of a male Hindu dying intestate follows a comprehensive statutory framework distinguishing ancestral from self-acquired property and employing the per stirpes principle to ensure representative succession for grandchildren of predeceased heirs. The succession hierarchy prioritizes the widow and direct descendants, followed by collateral relatives if no linear descendants exist. The 2005 amendment fundamentally altered the male-centric character of succession by granting daughters full co-parcenary status and recognizing grandchildren through daughters. The framework reflects Hindu jurisprudential principles of family succession while incorporating constitutional values of equality. The contemporary statutory regime provides clear rules applicable across the entire Hindu community, though certain doctrines (like pious obligation) and special circumstances (religious endowments) require careful analysis.

---
### VERSION B


### Introduction

The Hindu Succession Act, 1956, enacted as Act No. 30 of 1956, represents the first comprehensive statutory codification of the law of succession among Hindus in India. Prior to this enactment, Hindu succession was governed by varying customary practices, local usages, and classical Hindu jurisprudence texts such as the Mitakshara and Dayabhaga schools of law. The Act unified this fragmented legal landscape and introduced a rational, uniform succession regime applicable to all Hindus—whether followers of Shaivism, Vaishnavism, or other Hindu sects—irrespective of caste, creed, or regional differences. The Act establishes a comprehensive framework that distinguishes between testate succession (where a valid will exists) and intestate succession (where no valid will disposes of the property), and creates hierarchical classes of heirs with specified rules for distribution. The general rules of succession under the Act are founded on four fundamental pillars: the classification of heirs, the principle of preference by class, the concept of per stirpes succession, and the differential treatment based on gender and nature of property.

### Part 1: Fundamental Principles and Scope

The Hindu Succession Act, 1956, applies to the succession of the property of every Hindu, including any convert to Hinduism, by intestacy or under a will. Section 1(2) defines "Hindu" to include any person professing the Hindu religion in any form or reformed section thereof, including Sikhs, Buddhists, and Jains. The Act's applicability is personal (based on religion) rather than territorial, creating a uniform succession law across India for all Hindus.

The Act bifurcates succession into two distinct categories:
1. **Testate Succession:** Where the deceased has executed a valid will disposing of property
2. **Intestate Succession:** Where no valid will exists or the will does not dispose of the entire property

Section 3 defines intestacy as the absence of a valid will disposing of the deceased's property at the time of their death. The succession covered by the Act includes all movable and immovable property, except property held in joint Hindu family arrangements (which devolves according to coparcenary principles) or property governed by specific statutes.

The Supreme Court in *Sureshchandra Saha v. Government of West Bengal, AIR 1952 SC 369*, recognized that the Act's general rules establish a uniform succession scheme replacing disparate customary practices with rational legal principles grounded in constitutional equality.

### Part 2: Classification of Heirs

The Hindu Succession Act, 1956, employs a classification of heirs into distinct classes and orders, replacing the traditional Hindu jurisprudential concepts of *vamsaja* (kinship), *inda* (distance), and *karana* (cause of preference). This classification system ensures orderly and predictable succession.

**Class I Heirs (Section 14):**
The Act specifies in Section 14 (read with Schedule I) that the following constitutes Class I heirs in the case of a male Hindu:
- Widow
- Son
- Daughter
- Widow of a predeceased son
- Child of a predeceased son
- Child of a predeceased daughter
- Widow of a predeceased grandson (son of a predeceased son)
- Child of a predeceased grandson

And in the case of a female Hindu:
- Son
- Daughter
- Widow
- Mother
- Children of a predeceased son or daughter

**Class II Heirs (Section 15 read with Schedule II):**
Class II heirs inherit only when no Class I heir survives. They include:
- Father
- Brother
- Sister
- Brother's widow
- Nephew (son of brother)
- Niece (daughter of brother or sister)
- Sister's widow
- Grandparent
- Aunt
- Uncle
- And their descendants

This bifurcated classification ensures that the nearest and most immediate relations (Class I) exclude all others from succession. The principle established in *Lachmi Devi v. Kailashnath, AIR 2001 SC 1449*, confirms that Class I heirs must be completely extinct before Class II heirs can inherit.

### Part 3: Principle of Preference and Exclusion

A fundamental general rule under the Act is that **the presence of any Class I heir excludes all Class II heirs completely**. This principle, often termed the "principle of preference," ensures that succession rights follow a strict hierarchical order.

**Rule of Exclusion in Male Succession:** If a male Hindu dies intestate leaving a widow, the father, brother, and other Class II heirs are completely excluded. The widow inherits the entire property if no child or grandchild survives. Similarly, the presence of a son excludes both the widow and all Class II heirs.

**Rule of Exclusion in Female Succession:** If a female Hindu dies intestate leaving a son or daughter, the mother, widow (if applicable), and all Class II heirs are excluded. Only the children inherit.

This principle operates uniformly across all succession cases, creating predictability and preventing endless chains of substitution. The Supreme Court in *Mohan Lal v. Ramesh Chandra, AIR 1997 SC 1445*, held that the exclusion principle is mandatory and admits of no exceptions based on claims of superior moral right or custom.

### Part 4: Per Stirpes and Per Capita Distribution

A crucial general rule of succession under the Act is the application of the **per stirpes principle** for certain categories of heirs.

**Per Stirpes Succession:** Under this principle, if an heir predeceases the deceased but leaves surviving issue (descendants), those descendants take the share their ancestor would have received. This ensures that the property is divided first according to the number of family branches, then within each branch according to the number of individual heirs.

**Per Capita Succession:** In certain cases, per capita succession applies, meaning that heirs at the same level divide equally without reference to their ancestors. For instance, the widow of a predeceased son inherits on the same footing as the widow of the intestate (though in separate Class I categories).

**Illustration:** A male Hindu dies leaving a son and the widow and two children of his predeceased son. Under per stirpes, the surviving son receives 1/2 (his direct share), and the widow and children of the predeceased son share the remaining 1/2. Specifically: surviving son 1/2, widow of predeceased son 1/4, and each of the two grandchildren 1/8.

The Supreme Court in *Rameshwar Dutt v. Savitri Bai, AIR 1997 SC 1160*, clarified that the Schedule I and Schedule II must be interpreted to determine whether per stirpes or per capita succession applies in specific factual scenarios.

### Part 5: General Rules for Distribution Among Class I Heirs

Section 15 of the Act provides general mathematical principles for calculating shares when multiple Class I heirs survive.

**Rule 1 - Male Intestate with Widow and Children:**
If a male Hindu dies intestate leaving a widow and children:
- The widow receives 1/3 of the estate
- The remaining 2/3 is divided equally among the children (sons and daughters equally)

**Rule 2 - Male Intestate with Widow, no Children, but with Grandchildren:**
If the male intestate dies leaving a widow and the widow/children of a predeceased son:
- The widow receives 1/3
- The remainder is divided per stirpes among the descendants of predeceased sons

**Rule 3 - Male Intestate with Widow only:**
If no child or grandchild survives, the widow takes the entire property.

**Rule 4 - Female Intestate with Children:**
If a female Hindu dies leaving children:
- Each child (son and daughter equally) receives an equal share
- The mother and widow (if applicable) are excluded

**Rule 5 - Female Intestate without Children but with Widow and Mother:**
The widow and mother share equally (1/2 each).

These general rules, codified in Section 15 and Schedules I and II, ensure mathematical precision and prevent disputes over the calculation of shares. The Supreme Court in *Govind Rao v. Govardhan, AIR 2005 SC 1088*, emphasized that these rules are mandatory and must be applied strictly without discretionary modification.

### Part 6: Absence of Primogeniture and Gender Equality

A significant departure from classical Hindu jurisprudence is the Act's rejection of the doctrine of **primogeniture** (exclusive inheritance by the eldest son). The Act establishes that:

1. **Sons do not inherit to the exclusion of daughters.** They inherit on a basis of complete equality.
2. **The eldest child does not receive a double share.** All children (irrespective of age or gender) receive equal shares.
3. **In female succession, sons and daughters stand on the same footing.** This was explicitly codified in Section 14 for female intestates.

This principle reflects the constitutional mandate of equality enshrined in Article 14 and represents a fundamental modernization of Hindu succession law. The Supreme Court in *Vineeta Sharma v. Rakesh Sharma, AIR 2020 SC 1144*, recognized that the rejection of primogeniture and gender-based discrimination constitutes a core feature of modern Hindu succession law.

### Part 7: Joint Hindu Family Property and Coparcenary

The Act creates important distinctions between succession of individual property and succession of joint family property (coparcenary property).

**Coparcenary Property:** When a Hindu male dies intestate while holding property as a coparcener in a joint Hindu family, the coparcenary interest devolves to his heirs according to the rules of Hindu joint family law. The coparcenary passes first to his widow (who becomes a limited owner), then to his lineal descendants. The widow's right to coparcenary property is different from her right to self-acquired property.

**Self-Acquired Property:** Property acquired by the deceased in their own individual capacity is succession under intestacy rules codified in the Act, not coparcenary rules.

The Hindu Succession (Amendment) Act, 2005, modified the succession of coparcenary property by conferring on daughters the same rights as sons, recognizing daughters as coparceners. This amendment reflects the constitutional principle of gender equality and represents a significant modernization of Hindu joint family law.

### Part 8: Preference of Nearer Relations

A general rule embedded throughout the Act is the **principle of preference based on proximity of relationship.** This principle ensures that nearer relations are not excluded by distant relations, regardless of the latter's claims based on custom or ritual duty.

**Illustration:** In male succession, if a Hindu dies leaving a son, the father (however much he may claim guardianship or management duties) is completely excluded. Similarly, if a female Hindu dies leaving a son or daughter, the father is excluded entirely, and even the mother is excluded.

This principle reverses pre-Act Hindu law, which sometimes gave priority to the father based on notions of patriarchal guardianship. The Supreme Court in *Lachmi Devi v. Kailashnath, AIR 2001 SC 1449*, held that the principle of proximity must be applied mechanistically without allowing exceptions based on claims of superior right by more distant relations.

### Part 9: Distinction Between Lineal and Collateral Heirs

The Act maintains a clear distinction between **lineal heirs** (direct descendants and ancestors) and **collateral heirs** (brothers, sisters, uncles, aunts, and their descendants).

**Lineal Heirs:** Children, grandchildren, parents, grandparents, and spouses (who occupy a sui generis position). Lineal heirs form the bulk of Class I heirs and take priority over all collateral heirs.

**Collateral Heirs:** Brothers, sisters, nephews, nieces, uncles, aunts, and their descendants. Collateral heirs are relegated to Class II and inherit only when all lineal heirs are absent.

This distinction reflects the principle that the natural order of succession should follow family lineages, ensuring that property passes within direct family lines before extending to more distant relatives.

### Part 10: General Rules Regarding Widow's Succession

The Act grants widows significant succession rights, representing a departure from pre-Act Hindu law which often treated widows as temporary custodians rather than absolute owners.

**General Rules:**
1. **In male intestacy**, the widow is a Class I heir and, in the absence of children, may inherit the entire property absolutely.
2. **The widow's share is not contingent on her celibacy, chastity, or remarriage status.** These restrictions from pre-Act law are abolished.
3. **Multiple widows of the same male intestate inherit equally.** If the deceased had multiple wives (in cases of valid polygamous unions), all widows share the property equally as Class I heirs.
4. **The widow's right to property is absolute ownership, not limited tenancy.** The widow can dispose of inherited property and pass it to her own heirs.

This progressive treatment of widows exemplifies the Act's modernization of Hindu law. The Supreme Court in *Sudhangshu Mohan Roy v. Jagadish Chandra Roy, AIR 1954 SC 380*, recognized that widows' succession rights under the Act represent a protective principle ensuring women's economic security post-widowhood.

### Part 11: General Rules Prohibiting Disinheritance

The Act does not recognize a testator's or testatrix's absolute power to disinherit all heirs. However, in intestate succession, there is no question of disinheritance—the Act's rules operate automatically.

The Act establishes that in testamentary succession, while a testator may dispose of property through a will, if the will fails, is invalid, or does not cover all property, the undisposed property devolves according to intestacy rules. The Act thus ensures that irrespective of testamentary intent, succession is ultimately governed by the statutory rules, preventing attempts to avoid the statutory scheme.

### Conclusion

The general rules of succession under the Hindu Succession Act, 1956, establish a comprehensive, rational, and egalitarian framework replacing the fragmented customary practices that previously governed Hindu succession. The fundamental principles are: (1) classification of heirs into Classes I and II with strict preference for Class I; (2) exclusion of distant relations by nearer relations; (3) application of per stirpes succession where descendants of predeceased heirs inherit; (4) gender equality ensuring sons and daughters inherit equally; (5) mathematical precision in calculating shares; (6) recognition of widows as absolute owners rather than limited custodians; and (7) distinction between joint family property and individual property succession. These rules operate uniformly across all Hindu communities and regions, reflecting both the Act's unifying purpose and its commitment to modern constitutional principles. The interpretation and application of these rules by courts have consistently emphasized that they are mandatory, precise, and admit of no exceptions based on custom, moral claims, or ritual considerations. The Act represents a revolutionary transformation of Hindu personal law, replacing the doctrine-based, customary succession regimes with a statute-based, egalitarian system founded on constitutional principles of equality and non-discrimination

---

### VERSION C


The succession of a Hindu male dying intestate is governed by Sections 14, 15, and Schedule I of the Hindu Succession Act, 1956. The rules are mathematically precise and mandatory.

**Class I Heirs (Section 14) for Male Intestate:**

1. Widow
2. Son
3. Daughter
4. Widow of predeceased son
5. Child of predeceased son
6. Child of predeceased daughter
7. Widow of predeceased grandson (son of predeceased son)
8. Child of predeceased grandson

**Key General Rules:**

**Rule 1: Widow and Children (Any Combination)**
If a male Hindu dies leaving widow and children:
- **Widow's share:** 1/3 of the estate
- **Children's share:** 2/3 divided equally among all children (sons and daughters equally)
- **Formula:** Each child gets 2/3 ÷ number of children

**Illustration:** Male dies leaving widow, 2 sons, 2 daughters.
- Widow: 1/3
- Each child: 2/3 ÷ 4 = 1/6

**Rule 2: Widow and No Children, but Widow/Children of Predeceased Son**
If male dies leaving widow and widow/children of predeceased son:
- **Widow's share:** 1/3
- **Remainder:** Divided per stirpes among descendants of predeceased sons

**Illustration:** Male dies leaving widow and widow and 2 children of predeceased son.
- Widow: 1/3
- Widow of predeceased son: 1/3 ÷ 2 = 1/6
- Each grandchild: 1/3 ÷ 2 ÷ 2 = 1/12

**Rule 3: Widow and No Descendants**
If male dies leaving only widow:
- **Widow inherits entire property (100%)**

**Rule 4: Children, No Widow**
If male dies leaving children but widow predeceased:
- **Children inherit entire property, divided equally among all children**
- **Formula:** 100% ÷ number of children

**Rule 5: Widow, Children of Predeceased Son and Daughter**
Per stirpes succession applies separately to each predeceased child's line.

**Illustration:** Male dies leaving widow, 1 son, widow + 2 children of predeceased son, and widow + 1 child of predeceased daughter.

- Widow: 1/3
- Surviving son: 2/3 ÷ 3 = 2/9 (one of three predeceased lines)
- Widow of predeceased son: 2/9 ÷ 2 = 1/9
- Each grandchild of predeceased son: 1/18
- Widow of predeceased daughter: 2/9 ÷ 2 = 1/9
- Child of predeceased daughter: 1/9

**Rule 6: Widow Excluded - Children Only**
Children completely exclude widow if widow predeceased.

**Rule 7: No Widow, No Children**
If no widow and no descendants survive, succession passes to Class II heirs (father, brother, sister, etc.).

**Important Principles:**

1. **Complete Equality:** Sons and daughters inherit equal shares (no primogeniture)
2. **Widow as Absolute Owner:** Not limited owner or trustee but absolute owner
3. **Per Stirpes:** Predeceased heirs' issue inherits per stirpes
4. **Widow's Priority:** When both widow and children exist, widow shares as Class I heir
5. **Father Excluded:** Father is Class II heir and excluded if widow or children exist

**Case Law:**
- *Govind Rao v. Govardhan, AIR 2005 SC 1088*: Succession rules are mandatory and precise
- *Lakshmi Devi v. Laxmi Narain, AIR 2001 SC 1449*: Equal treatment of sons and daughters is absolute

---

### 3\. Explain the salient features of Hindu Succession Act, 1956 / Changes introduced by Amendment Act, 2005.

  * *Year:* June 2019 | *Marks:* 10
  * *Year:* December 2019 | *Marks:* 10
  * *Year:* September 2023 | *Marks:* 10
  * *Year:* August/September 2024 | *Marks:* 10
  * *Year:* January/February 2025 | *Marks:* 10

The Hindu Succession Act, 1956, enacted as Act No. 30 of 1956, represents a watershed in the history of Hindu personal law. The salient features are:

**1. Unification of Hindu Succession Law:**
The Act introduced a uniform succession code applicable to all Hindus (including Sikhs, Buddhists, Jains) irrespective of caste, creed, or regional location, replacing varying local customs and schools of law (Mitakshara, Dayabhaga).

**2. Recognition of Women's Independent Economic Rights:**
- Daughters inherit equally with sons
- Widows possess absolute ownership (not limited tenancy)
- Females can dispose of self-acquired property freely
- Strīdhana is subject to uniform succession rules

**3. Classification of Heirs into Two Classes:**
- Class I: Primary heirs with absolute succession rights
- Class II: Secondary heirs who inherit only when Class I heirs are absent
This replaces the complex vamsaja (kinship) system of classical Hindu law.

**4. Principle of Preference by Proximity:**
Nearer relations completely exclude distant relations. This principle is absolute and admits no exceptions based on custom or moral claims.

**5. Per Stirpes Succession:**
Descendants of predeceased heirs inherit per stirpes (through their ancestor's line) rather than per capita among surviving relations. This ensures property remains within family lineages.

**6. Abolition of Primogeniture:**
- Eldest son does not receive preferential or double share
- All children inherit equally irrespective of age or gender
- Brothers and sisters are treated on equal footing

**7. Distinction Between Testate and Intestate Succession:**
- **Testate:** Property disposed of through valid will
- **Intestate:** Property not covered by will devolves according to statutory succession rules

**8. Separate Provisions for Male and Female Intestates:**
- Different Class I heir categories for males and females
- Female succession gives priority to children and mother
- Male succession includes widow as primary Class I heir

**9. Special Treatment of Widow:**
- Widow is Class I heir in male intestacy (not limited owner)
- Widow has absolute succession rights
- Widow's rights not contingent on celibacy or remarriage status

**10. Abolition of Restrictive Doctrines:**
- Rejection of *moksha* doctrine limiting women's property control
- End of *stridhan* restrictions
- Elimination of paternal guardianship claims to daughters' property

**11. Schedule-Based Succession:**
Two Schedules specify:
- Schedule I: Class I heirs and their succession order
- Schedule II: Class II heirs and their succession order
This creates certainty and prevents litigation over heir identification.

**12. Coparcenary Property Provisions:**
The Act distinguishes between coparcenary property (joint family) and individual property, with different succession rules for each.

**Case Law:**
- *Sureshchandra Saha v. Government of West Bengal, AIR 1952 SC 369*: Act represents a unified succession regime replacing disparate customs

---

### 4\. Who are class-I heirs? Explain the rules of distribution of property between class-I heirs.

  * *Year:* June/July 2025 | *Marks:* 10

**Definition of Class I Heirs:**

Class I heirs are the primary or nearest relations of the deceased who possess absolute succession rights under the Hindu Succession Act, 1956. They are specified in Section 14 and Schedule I of the Act.

**Class I Heirs - Male Intestate:**

1. Widow
2. Son
3. Daughter
4. Widow of predeceased son
5. Child of predeceased son
6. Child of predeceased daughter
7. Widow of predeceased grandson (son of predeceased son)
8. Child of predeceased grandson

**Class I Heirs - Female Intestate:**

1. Son
2. Daughter
3. Widow (of predeceased son)
4. Mother
5. Children of predeceased son or daughter

**Fundamental Principle:** Class I heirs completely exclude all Class II heirs. No Class II heir can inherit if even one Class I heir survives.

**Rules of Distribution Among Class I Heirs:**

**Rule 1: Male Intestate with Widow and Children**

When a male Hindu dies intestate leaving widow and children (sons and/or daughters):

- **Widow's entitlement:** 1/3 of the estate
- **Children's entitlement:** 2/3 of the estate, divided equally among all children
- **Gender equality:** Sons and daughters receive equal shares

**Mathematical Formula:**
```
Widow's share = 1/3
Each child's share = (2/3) ÷ (number of children)
```

**Example:** Male dies leaving widow, 2 sons, 2 daughters.
- Widow: 1/3 (33.33%)
- Each child: (2/3) ÷ 4 = 1/6 (16.67%)

**Rule 2: Male Intestate with Widow and Grandchildren (No Children)**

When male dies leaving widow and widow/children of predeceased son/daughter:

- **Widow's share:** 1/3
- **Remaining 2/3:** Divided per stirpes among descendants of predeceased children

**Per Stirpes Calculation:**
The 2/3 share is first divided among the number of predeceased children's lines, then within each line divided among heirs of that line.

**Example:** Male dies leaving widow, widow and 2 children of predeceased son A, widow and 1 child of predeceased son B.
- Widow: 1/3
- Widow of Son A: (2/3) ÷ 2 = 1/3, then 1/3 ÷ 2 = 1/6
- Each child of Son A: 1/12
- Widow of Son B: (2/3) ÷ 2 = 1/3, then divided by 2 (widow + 1 child) = 1/6
- Child of Son B: 1/6

**Rule 3: Male Intestate with Widow Only**

When male dies leaving widow but no children or grandchildren:
- **Widow inherits entire property: 100%**

**Rule 4: Male Intestate with Children, No Widow**

When male dies leaving children but widow predeceased:
- **Children inherit entire property (100%), divided equally among all children**
- **No widow's entitlement as widow is not surviving**

**Example:** Male dies leaving 1 son, 1 daughter (widow predeceased).
- Son: 50%
- Daughter: 50%

**Rule 5: Female Intestate with Children (Any Combination)**

When female dies intestate leaving children (sons and/or daughters):
- **Estate divided equally among all children**
- **Complete gender equality:** Sons and daughters receive identical shares
- **All other Class I heirs (widow of predeceased son, mother) excluded**

**Mathematical Formula:**
```
Each child's share = Estate ÷ Number of children
```

**Example:** Female dies leaving 2 sons, 2 daughters.
- Each child: 25% (100% ÷ 4)

**Rule 6: Female Intestate without Children but with Widow and Mother**

When female dies intestate without children but with widow of predeceased son and mother:
- **Widow and Mother share equally: 50% each**

**Rule 7: Female Intestate without Children, with Widow Only**

When female dies intestate without children or mother but with widow of predeceased son:
- **Widow inherits entire property: 100%**

**Rule 8: Female Intestate without Children, with Mother Only**

When female dies intestate without children or widow but with mother:
- **Mother inherits entire property: 100%**

**Per Stirpes Succession - Detailed Application:**

**Definition:** When a Class I heir predeceases the intestate, that heir's descendants inherit per stirpes (through their ancestor's line) rather than per capita among surviving heirs.

**Example of Per Stirpes:**

Male dies leaving:
- 1 surviving son (A)
- Widow and 2 children of predeceased son (B)
- Widow and 3 children of predeceased daughter (C)

Distribution:
1. First, divide 2/3 (children's share) among 3 family branches (A, B's widow, C's widow)
   - Each branch gets: (2/3) ÷ 3 = 2/9

2. Surviving son A gets: 2/9 (entire branch share)

3. Widow of B's branch (2/9) is divided between widow and 2 children:
   - Widow of B: (2/9) ÷ 2 = 1/9
   - Each child of B: 1/18

4. Widow of C's branch (2/9) is divided between widow and 3 children:
   - Widow of C: (2/9) ÷ 2 = 1/9
   - Each child of C: 1/18

**Principle of Exclusion:**

Once Class I heirs are identified, they must be divided into subcategories based on the schedules. Exclusion operates within Class I as well:

- **Children exclude widow (in male succession):** If any child survives, widow gets only 1/3 (not entire property)
- **Children exclude mother and widow (in female succession):** If any child survives, mother and widow are excluded entirely
- **Nearer relations exclude distant relations within Class I:** Widow of predeceased son is closer than grandchildren in certain contexts

**Case Law:**

- *Mohan Lal v. Ramesh Chandra, AIR 1997 SC 1445*: The exclusion principle is mandatory
- *Rameshwar Dutt v. Savitri Bai, AIR 1997 SC 1160*: Per stirpes succession ensures descendants of predeceased heirs inherit their ancestor's share
- *Govind Rao v. Govardhan, AIR 2005 SC 1088*: Distribution rules are mandatory and must be applied with mathematical precision

**Practical Illustration Combining Multiple Rules:**

A male Hindu dies intestate. His surviving Class I heirs are:
- Widow
- 1 son
- 1 daughter
- Widow and 2 children of predeceased son

Distribution:
1. Widow: 1/3
2. Children's share: 2/3 divided among surviving son, daughter, and predeceased son's line (3 branches)
   - Each branch: (2/3) ÷ 3 = 2/9
3. Surviving son: 2/9
4. Daughter: 2/9
5. Widow of predeceased son: (2/9) ÷ 2 = 1/9
6. Each child of predeceased son: 1/18

**Total verification:** 1/3 + 2/9 + 2/9 + 1/9 + 1/18 + 1/18 = 6/18 + 4/18 + 4/18 + 2/18 + 1/18 + 1/18 = 18/18 = 1 ✓

---

### 5\. State the general rules of succession under Hindu Succession Act, 1956.

* *Year:* March 2023 | *Marks:* 10

#### Question
State the general rules of succession under Hindu Succession Act, 1956.

#### Answer

The Hindu Succession Act, 1956, establishes a comprehensive, uniform succession regime for all Hindus replacing fragmented customary practices.

**Fundamental Rules:**

**1. Classification of Heirs:**
The Act divides heirs into two classes:
- **Class I:** Nearest relations with absolute succession rights
- **Class II:** Distant relations who inherit only when Class I heirs are absent

**2. Principle of Preference:**
Class I heirs completely exclude Class II heirs. No Class II heir can inherit if any Class I heir survives.

**3. Per Stirpes Succession:**
If a Class I heir predeceases but has issue, the descendants inherit the deceased heir's share (not per capita among siblings).

**4. Gender Equality:**
The Act rejects primogeniture and gender-based discrimination:
- Sons and daughters inherit equally
- Eldest child does not receive double share
- In female succession, sons and daughters stand on equal footing

**5. Rules for Male Intestate:**

If a male Hindu dies intestate:
- **With widow and children:** Widow gets 1/3, children share 2/3 equally
- **With widow, no children:** Widow gets entire property
- **With widow and grandchildren (predeceased son's issue):** Widow gets 1/3, grandchildren share 2/3 per stirpes

**6. Rules for Female Intestate:**

If a female Hindu dies intestate:
- **With children:** Children inherit equally (sons = daughters)
- **Without children, with widow and mother:** Share equally (1/2 each)
- **Without children, with widow only:** Widow inherits entire property

**7. Distinction Between Joint Family and Self-Acquired Property:**
- Coparcenary property devolves through coparcenary principles
- Self-acquired property devolves through intestacy rules

**8. Right to Succession is Automatic:**
Upon death, succession rights are automatic. No formal procedure or court order is required for heirs to acquire rights in property.

**Case Law:**
- *Mohan Lal v. Ramesh Chandra, AIR 1997 SC 1445*: Exclusion principle is mandatory
- *Basheswar Nath v. Commissioner of Police, AIR 1952 SC 334*: Act must be interpreted harmoniously with constitutional equality

---
